    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="4450274bb09e4839b83ee64b66447b23">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '3ae73698-68d6-46b4-8e39-ae26c9ab58a5'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d90324388" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p><b>Reduced to clear</b>: A small amount of graphite unexpectedly acts as the catalyst to greatly promote the microwave exfoliation and reduction of graphite oxide (GO) in ambient air. The catalytic microwave exfoliated graphite oxide (CMEGO) product has ultrahigh electrochemical performances as anodes for both lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/62a924aa-67dc-44ea-811a-ab5bd0725a8b/anie201708714-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/62a924aa-67dc-44ea-811a-ab5bd0725a8b/anie201708714-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d50d1f3e-9966-432e-b342-09e977e9cfb7/anie201708714-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/62a924aa-67dc-44ea-811a-ab5bd0725a8b/anie201708714-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d90324391" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Herein we report that a small amount of graphite can unexpectedly act as the catalyst to greatly promote the microwave exfoliation and reduction of graphite oxide in ambient air. The reaction can be finished in a few seconds in contrast to more than ten minutes without catalyst. The catalytic microwave exfoliated graphite oxide (CMEGO) is of higher quality than the traditional microwave exfoliated graphite oxide, including a much higher exfoliation degree with thinner graphene sheets and higher specific surface area (886 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> vs. 466 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>), a much larger C/O ratio (19.4 vs. 6.3) and a higher lattice crystallinity, as well as significantly improved electrical conductivity (53180 S m<sup>−1</sup> vs. 5140 S m<sup>−1</sup>). The CMEGO is used as anode for lithium-ion battery (LIB) and sodium-ion battery (SIB), and delivers ultrahigh reversible capacities, remarkable rate capabilities, and superior cycling stabilities in both LIB and SIB.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie201708714-sec-0001">
         
         <p>As a unique two-dimensional single-atom-thick carbon sheet, graphene has been anticipated to revolutionize a wide range of technological areas owing to its multiple fascinating physical–chemical properties.<span><sup><a href="#anie201708714-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0001_R_d90324380e340" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science">1</a>-<a href="#anie201708714-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0003_R_d90324380e340" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Science">3</a></sup></span> Production of high-quality graphene flakes on a large scale is the key step to the practical application of graphene in many fields. Several typical methods, such as micromechanical cleavage, chemical vapor deposition, and exfoliation and reduction of graphite oxide (GO) have been widely used to yield graphene for basic and applied research.<span><sup><a href="#anie201708714-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0001_R_d90324380e342" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science">1</a></sup></span>,<span><sup><a href="#anie201708714-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0004_R_d90324380e344" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Mater.">4</a>-<a href="#anie201708714-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0006_R_d90324380e344" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">6</a></sup></span> Among these, exfoliation and reduction of GO has attracted intense interest among chemists and materials scientists owing to its capability for affordable and high-throughput production of reduced graphene oxide (RGO) or chemically converted graphene.<span><sup><a href="#anie201708714-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0007_R_d90324380e346" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Carbon">7</a>-<a href="#anie201708714-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0010_R_d90324380e346" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Science">10</a></sup></span> The typical reduction methods of GO include chemical reduction and high-temperature thermal expansion, which involve either potentially hazardous reducing agents or high energy consumption.<span><sup><a href="#anie201708714-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0011_R_d90324380e348" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Carbon">11</a>, <a href="#anie201708714-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0012_R_d90324380e348" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">12</a></sup></span> Recently, microwave irradiation has been introduced as a rapid and convenient method to simultaneously achieve the exfoliation and reduction of GO.<span><sup><a href="#anie201708714-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0013_R_d90324380e351" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Mater. Chem.">13</a>-<a href="#anie201708714-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0015_R_d90324380e351" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Nano">15</a></sup></span> For example, Ruoff et al. first reported a straightforward method by treating the GO powder directly in a commercial microwave oven to prepare electrically conductive (274 S m<sup>−1</sup>) microwave exfoliated graphite oxide (MEGO) with a moderate deoxygenation degree (2.75 C/O ratio) and specific surface area (SSA) (463 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) which could be used as promising supercapacitor electrode material (191 F g<sup>−1</sup>).<span><sup><a href="#anie201708714-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0014_R_d90324380e361" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Carbon">14</a></sup></span> Despite this progress in recent years, it is still highly desired to develop new methods for fast mass production of high-quality graphene involving less- or non-toxic chemicals.</p>
         
         <p>Herein we report a universal, environmental friendly, and ultrafast catalytic microwave method that is a combination of the simplicity, high quality, and efficiency by using a small amount of flake graphite as the catalyst which can unexpectedly promote microwave exfoliation and reduction of GO significantly. This catalytic microwave method can shorten the reaction time from more than ten minutes without catalyst to a few seconds in ambient air and generate what we called catalytic microwave exfoliated graphite oxide (CMEGO) with ultrahigh SSA (886 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>), large C/O ratio (19.4), low lattice defects, and significant electrical conductivity (53 180 S m<sup>−1</sup>) as well as excellent solvent dispersability and processability. Owing to the intrinsic structure advantages of CMEGO, when using as an anode material for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), it delivered ultrahigh reversible capacities of 2260 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 469 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a charge/discharge rate of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, and showed an outstanding capacity retention of 91.4 % after 1000 cycles at a high current density of 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Furthermore, CMEGO also demonstrated remarkable electrochemical performance as an anode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), including very high reversible capacities of 424 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 218 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, and a stable capacity retention of 85.7 % after 1000 cycles at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Such superior electrochemical performances achieved by a single graphene anode material for both LIB and SIB have rarely been demonstrated previously, and both the performances of CMEGO are much better than those of MEGO and among the best results achieved by various graphene materials reported to date.</p>
         
         <p>Typically, the as-prepared GO powder (200 mg) was first irradiated by microwave (1000 W) to yield MEGO using the reported method. It usually takes as long as 15 min without any visible phenomenon and then suddenly finished with a brief explosion all at a moment (Video 1 in the Supporting Information). The covered flask bounded up at the end proving a violent gas-releasing process. The phenomena are similar to previous reports.<span><sup><a href="#anie201708714-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0014_R_d90324380e397" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Carbon">14</a></sup></span> To our surprise, this reaction could be triggered immediately and finished within 5 s when very little flake graphite powder (even less than 1 mg) was added on the GO powder. Upon microwave irradiation, the volume of CMEGO was significantly enlarged (almost double than that of MEGO, Figure <a href="#anie201708714-fig-0001">1</a>) accompanied with no explosion but a “shining violent flash” (Figure S1 and Video 2 in the Supporting Information). As illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie201708714-fig-0001">1</a>, the mechanism of this catalytic microwave process is probably because the flake graphite powder with highly extended π-system can efficiently absorb microwave as a receptor<span><sup><a href="#anie201708714-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0016_R_d90324380e403" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Carbon">16</a>-<a href="#anie201708714-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0018_R_d90324380e403" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Carbon">18</a></sup></span> and convert the energy into nearby gas molecules so that the gas molecules were activated and then plasma occurred which created a local ultrahigh-energy environment and reduced graphite oxide instantly. The proposed mechanism could be supported by the observed phenomenon that when CuO was treated in microwave oven protected by Argon atmosphere, the CuO absorbed microwave and was converted to Cu<sub>2</sub>O. As is well established, this conversion reaction requires high temperature well above 1000 °C, which indicates that the adsorption of microwave can create high energy environment (Figure S2b). This simple and superfast catalytic microwave method thus allows scalable preparation of MEGO conveniently (Figure <a href="#anie201708714-fig-0001">1</a>, right), which is highly desired for industrial application.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201708714-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d4a98e4c-f00f-426f-adf4-cfd44c39321c/anie201708714-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d4a98e4c-f00f-426f-adf4-cfd44c39321c/anie201708714-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8aceb54f-bd3e-4869-b65c-d8f917c3abe3/anie201708714-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d4a98e4c-f00f-426f-adf4-cfd44c39321c/anie201708714-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201708714-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201708714" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The synthesis process of MEGO and CMEGO.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>We performed detailed characterizations on the CMEGO and MEGO. Firstly, FT-IR spectra of GO, MEGO, CMEGO, and graphite was shown in Figure <a href="#anie201708714-fig-0002">2</a> a. Normally, graphite shows no conspicuous peaks in FT-IR spectrum. The strong absorption peak at 3440, 1732, 1630, and 1055 cm<sup>−1</sup> of GO, corresponding to O−H, C=O, C−OH and C-O-C vibrations, respectively, are greatly reduced in MEGO and CMEGO, which indicating most of oxygen functional groups have been removed through the microwave and catalytic microwave process. When focused on CMEGO, we find that all peaks were weaker than MEGO, which indicated a better removal of oxygen functional groups. To further confirm the efficient reduction of GO, thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was also tested and shown in Figure <a href="#anie201708714-fig-0002">2</a> b. The GO showed a large weight loss upon heated to 800 °C owning to the removal of oxygen functional groups. Obviously, the TGA curves demonstrated that the weight reduction of CMEGO (ca. 7 % weight loss) is much smaller than that of MEGO (ca. 22 % weight loss) after heated to 800 °C, which indicates a better reduction of CMEGO and is consisted with the FT-IR result. The X-ray photoemission spectroscopy (XPS) survey spectra of GO, MEGO and CMEGO showed that the C/O atomic ratio was increased from 2.1 for GO to 6.8 and 17.4 for MEGO and CMEGO, respectively, which further support that CMEGO is much more reduced than MEGO by catalytic microwave reaction. The more detailed C1s spectra showed that the oxygen functional groups of CMEGO were removed more thoroughly than MEGO (Figure S3 in the Supporting Information). Combustion elemental analysis also revealed that the CMEGO has a much higher C/O ratio (19.4) than the MEGO (6.3) and previous various RGO samples (Table S1). We further employed Raman spectroscopy to provide an insight into the structural differences between MEGO and CMEGO. The MEGO suffers from a low reduction efficiency and remains highly disordered due to the absence of a distinct 2D band and the presence of an intense disorder D band in the Raman spectra (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>=1.38), similar to most of previous RGO samples. By contrast, the CMEGO exhibited highly ordered Raman features with sharp 2D peak and weaker D peak (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>=0.88).<span><sup><a href="#anie201708714-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0019_R_d90324380e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">19</a></sup></span> We infer that it is the absorption of microwaves by flake graphite and the formation of high-energy plasma that led to rapid heating of the GO, causing removal of oxygen functional groups in a moment and significant reordering of the graphene basal plane. A N<sub>2</sub> adsorption Brunauer-Emmett-Teller (BET) measurement on CMEGO powders showed an extremely high SSA of 886 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, nearly 2 times larger than that of MEGO (466 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>), which indicates a much higher exfoliation degree for CMEGO. All these results demonstrate that the exfoliation and reduction of GO in the ultrahigh energy environment created by catalytic microwave plasma can lead to a more complete removal of oxygen functional groups and a lower lattice defects as well as a higher specific surface area. Due to the better exfoliation, the CMEGO could be dispersed in DMF by ultrasonication for 5 min. Compared to MEGO with the same ultrasonic time, CMEGO showed a much better dispersal ability in organic solvent (Figure <a href="#anie201708714-fig-0002">2</a> f) with no precipitates observed even in one week. The excellent dispersability of CMEGO allows easy processing of CMRGO into a free-standing flexible film via vacuum filtration (Figure S4). The conductivity of CMEGO film is measured to be 48 662–53 180 S m<sup>−1</sup> which is much higher than MEGO film (3737–5140 S m<sup>−1</sup>) and also remarkably higher than many types of RGO prepared by different reduction methods (Table S2).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201708714-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9ee9dadc-b314-43a7-a566-596a5c27f69b/anie201708714-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9ee9dadc-b314-43a7-a566-596a5c27f69b/anie201708714-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/dfa743b3-881a-4bd0-9e03-fc52e5400bfa/anie201708714-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9ee9dadc-b314-43a7-a566-596a5c27f69b/anie201708714-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201708714-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201708714" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) FT-IR spectra of the GO, MEGO, CMEGO, and graphite. b) TGA curves for GO, CMEGO, and MEGO. c) XPS survey spectra of GO, MEGO, and CMEGO. d) Raman spectra of GO, MEGO, and CMEGO. e) Nitrogen adsorption and desorption isotherms for MEGO and CMEGO. f) Dispersity test of MEGO and CMEGO in DMF upon ultrasonic treatment for 5 min.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The morphology and microstructure of MEGO and CMEGO were further characterized by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). Compared with MEGO prepared by traditional microwave irradiation, the CMEGO is exfoliated more thoroughly with abundant thinner graphene sheets (Figure <a href="#anie201708714-fig-0003">3</a> a–d). The TEM images (Figure <a href="#anie201708714-fig-0003">3</a> e, f) indicated that the MEGO has micrometer-sized flakes with wrinkled and stacked graphene sheets, while the CMEGO showed more smooth and transparent sheets, further suggesting the exfoliation is better and the lattice distortion is weaker in CEMGO. We can readily obtain single sharp selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern for CMEGO compared with the multiplex blurred SAED pattern for MEGO (inset, Figure <a href="#anie201708714-fig-0003">3</a> e,f), indicating CMEGO has a better crystallinity than MEGO and the presence of single layer graphene in CMEGO, consistent with the Raman and BET results. Higher-magnification HRTEM images (Figure <a href="#anie201708714-fig-0003">3</a> g,h) show that the CMEGO has a more regular lattice and fewer graphene layers than the MEGO, consistent with the above results.<span><sup><a href="#anie201708714-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0020_R_d90324380e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Solid State Commun.">20</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201708714-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bbaa3b06-9aa0-491d-867c-ec49fb295c23/anie201708714-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/bbaa3b06-9aa0-491d-867c-ec49fb295c23/anie201708714-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/40425673-dcf5-4cb8-a1e0-4965a8f90d3e/anie201708714-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bbaa3b06-9aa0-491d-867c-ec49fb295c23/anie201708714-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201708714-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201708714" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a),b) SEM images of MEGO powder samples. c),d) SEM images of CMEGO powder samples. e),f) TEM images and the inset SAED pattern of MEGO and CMEGO samples, respectively. g),h) HR-TEM images of MEGO and CMEGO samples, respectively.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Graphene holds great promise in electrochemical energy storage but its potential is largely limited by the restacking and the defects in the bulk graphene materials.<span><sup><a href="#anie201708714-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0021_R_d90324380e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">21</a></sup></span> Owing to the multiple structural features of CMEGO, we further studied the electrochemical performance of CMEGO as a new anode for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs). The MEGO was also tested for comparison to understand the structure–property relationship. The electrodes were prepared by mixing 80 wt % of CMEGO or MEGO with 10 wt % of Super P carbon black and 10 wt % polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) binder. Figure <a href="#anie201708714-fig-0004">4</a> a,b show the galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of CMEGO and MEGO electrodes between 0.01 and 3 V at various current densities. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie201708714-fig-0004">4</a> c, the CMEGO electrode delivered a capacity of 6088 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in the initial discharging process and a reversible capacity of 2952 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in the first charging cycle with a Coulombic efficiency of 48.5 %. The irreversible capacity loss in the first cycle could be associated with electrolyte decomposition and the formation of a solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) on the electrode surface.<span><sup><a href="#anie201708714-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0021_R_d90324380e540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">21</a>, <a href="#anie201708714-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0022_R_d90324380e540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Nano">22</a></sup></span> The capacity of the CMEGO electrode gradually became stable after several charge/discharge cycles and the CMEGO electrode exhibited an ultrahigh reversible capacity of 2093 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 50 cycles, which is the best ever reported result for graphene anode materials for LIBs (Table S3). In contrast, the MEGO electrode showed much lower reversible capacities of 1204 and 932 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in the first cycle and after 50 cycles, respectively.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201708714-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d9aacaef-0970-4f5c-9494-68b7630187d0/anie201708714-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d9aacaef-0970-4f5c-9494-68b7630187d0/anie201708714-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/aef41f09-b84a-48c0-99aa-171d903af753/anie201708714-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d9aacaef-0970-4f5c-9494-68b7630187d0/anie201708714-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201708714-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201708714" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a),b) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of CMEGO (a) and MEGO (b) in LIBs. c) Cycle performance and Coulombic efficiency of the CMEGO and MEGO electrodes at a low current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> between 3.0 and 0.01 V versus Li<sup>+</sup>/Li. d) Rate capabilities and cycle performance of CMEGO and MEGO in LIBs. e) Cycle performance and Coulombic efficiency of the CMEGO electrode at a high current density of 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. f) Nyquist plots for the CMEGO and MEGO electrodes.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>We further studied the rate performance of both electrodes (Figure <a href="#anie201708714-fig-0004">4</a> d). When the current density was increased to 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 5.0, 10.0, 20.0, and 30.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the CMEGO electrode shows reversible capacities of 2060, 1524, 1132, 785, 600, 553, 469 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. However, the MEGO electrode could just deliver reversible capacities of 806, 612, 453, 308, 225, 209, 167 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, at the same test conditions. Even at a very high current density of 30.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, a stable reversible capacity of 469 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> could be reached by CMEGO electrode, which is nearly two-times higher than that of MEGO electrode (167 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and also higher than other graphene materials obtained before (Table S3). Moreover, when the current density returned to 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> immediately after 30.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, a remarkable reversible capacity of 2260 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> could be retained for CMEGO. Even after 1000 charge/discharge cycles at a high current density of 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the CMEGO can still deliver a high capacity of 740 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a significant retention of 91.4 % (Figure <a href="#anie201708714-fig-0004">4</a> e).</p>
         
         <p>All these results indicated that CMEGO could provide more favorable charge transport and ion diffusion processes than MEGO, which was further proved by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy. As could be seen in Figure <a href="#anie201708714-fig-0004">4</a> f, CMEGO exhibits a smaller semicircle diameter and a relatively shorter Warburg region which refers to a lower charge-transfer resistance and a much faster lithium-ion diffusion in CMEGO electrode.<span><sup><a href="#anie201708714-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0023_R_d90324380e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Angew. Chem.">23</a></sup></span> Based on the above comparison, the superior electrochemical performance of CMEGO could be attributed to the following points. First, the large SSA obtained in CMEGO by catalytic microwave treatment provides a larger electrode-electrolyte interface and exposed much more active sites for lithium-ion storage than MEGO. Second, the better exfoliation of CMEGO allows the lithium-ion diffusing process become more efficient and lithium ions could even reach the deeply buried locations within the stacked graphene layers. Typically, for graphene electrode materials, the capacity below 0.5 V (vs. Li<sup>+</sup>/Li) comes from lithium intercalation into the graphene layers, whereas the capacity above 0.5 V (vs. Li<sup>+</sup>/Li) is mainly associated with the Faradic capacitance on the surface or edge sites of the graphene sheets.<span><sup><a href="#anie201708714-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0024_R_d90324380e616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nano Lett.">24</a></sup></span> CMEGO not only has a better exfoliation to make lithium intercalation easier to realize, but also has a larger SSA to enlarge the Faradic capacitance on the surface sites.</p>
         
         <p>Because large-scale application of LIBs might be constrained by the uneven geographical distribution and high cost of lithium resources, SIBs is a promising alternative to LIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie201708714-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0025_R_d90324380e621" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Energy">25</a>, <a href="#anie201708714-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0026_R_d90324380e621" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nano Energy">26</a></sup></span> However, due to the larger ionic radius of sodium than lithium, SIBs requires relatively larger channels and interstitial sites for the transport and storage of sodium ions.<span><sup><a href="#anie201708714-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201708714-bib-0027_R_d90324380e623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Nano">27</a></sup></span> Therefore, many electrode materials such as graphite once suitable for LIBs fail to perform well in SIBs. Owing to the multiple advantageous features, we also studied the electrochemical performance of CMEGO as the anode for SIBs. Figure <a href="#anie201708714-fig-0005">5</a> a,b show the galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of CMEGO and MEGO electrodes at various current densities within a cutoff voltage window of 0.01–2.5 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie201708714-fig-0005">5</a> c, CMEGO delivered a high reversible capacity of 661 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 420 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in the first cycle and after 50 cycles, respectively, at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, much higher than those of MEGO (312 and 200 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively). Figure <a href="#anie201708714-fig-0005">5</a> d shows the rate performances of CMEGO and MEGO at different current densities. CMEGO electrode could be reversibly charged to 460, 375, 339, 261, 230, 195, 164, 148 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the current density of 0.1, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, 10, 20, 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Moreover, when the current density returned to 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> immediately after 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>, a reversible capacity of 408 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> could be recovered. In contrast, the MEGO showed much lower reversible capacities of only 270, 156, 116, 93, 75, 69, 65, 62 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the current densities of 0.1, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, 10, 20, 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Notice that even at 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>, a very high current density, CMEGO electrode could still maintain a capacity of 148 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, over two-times higher than MEGO, which can be attributed to more efficient ion transport in CMEGO confirmed by the EIS results (Figure <a href="#anie201708714-fig-0005">5</a> f). The CMEGO electrode also demonstrated an excellent cycling stability with a high capacity of 212 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and capacity retention of 85.7 % after 1000 charge/discharge cycles at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie201708714-fig-0005">5</a> e). It is worth noting that the electrochemical performance of CMEGO is not only much better than that of MEGO but also among the best results achieved for graphene anode materials for SIBs (Table S4). The reasons for such pleasant performance of CMEGO in SIBs could be largely similar to the above ones in LIBs: the CMEGO provides a large SSA and short sodium ion diffusion pathway as well as expanded intersheet distance for promotion of sodium ion interclation and adsorption.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201708714-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d218213a-0e04-4272-a5cf-5e0806d72d97/anie201708714-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d218213a-0e04-4272-a5cf-5e0806d72d97/anie201708714-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a35ae31b-e0a1-473d-9d57-b0a87d0a2cd2/anie201708714-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d218213a-0e04-4272-a5cf-5e0806d72d97/anie201708714-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201708714-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201708714" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a),b) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of CMEGO (a) and MEGO (b) in SIBs. c) Cycle performance and Coulombic efficiency of the CMEGO and MEGO electrode at a low current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> between 2.5 and 0.01 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. d) Rate capabilities and cycle performance of CMEGO and MEGO in SIBs. e) Cycle performance and Coulombic efficiency of the CMEGO electrode at a high current density of 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. f) Nyquist plots for the CMEGO and MEGO electrodes.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In summary, we report a simple, green, and ultrafast method for preparation of high quality graphene by using a very small amount of flake graphite as the unexpected effective catalyst to greatly promote exfoliation and reduction of GO in ambient air. The CMEGO obtained shows much better properties than the traditional MEGO, including a large SSA (886 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>), thinner graphene sheets, significant deoxygenation (19.4 C/O ratio) and low lattice defects as well as high electrical conductivity (over 53 000 S m<sup>−1</sup>). The CMEGO could be used as a high-performance anode material for both LIBs and SIBs simultaneously, which delivered ultrahigh reversible capacities of 2260 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 460 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> for LIBs and SIBs, respectively, and remarkable rate capabilities (469 and 148 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 30 A g<sup>−1</sup> for LIBs and SIBs, respectively) and superior cycling stabilities (91.4 % and 85.7 % for LIBs and SIBs, respectively). This catalytic microwave irradiation strategy opens a new pathway to large-scale synthesis of high quality graphene with promising applications in energy storage and conversion and beyond.</p>

                     <div class="extra-links getFTR"><span class="hidden data-doi">10.1126/science.1102896</span><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div>
